<div id="review_2546826724" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2546826724" />
    <a title="Alexei Ghertescu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27305898-alexei-ghertescu"><img alt="Alexei Ghertescu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1389029271p2/27305898.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2546826724?book_show_action=true">Nov 13, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alexei Ghertescu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alexei Ghertescu" href="/user/show/27305898-alexei-ghertescu">Alexei Ghertescu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2546826724" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer369481195055465667">I would recommend “Discipline and Punish: the Birth of the Prison” to everyone interested in the mechanisms of modern control of society through the institutions created since the end of the 18th century. <br/><br/>Michel Foucault is considered one of the major philosophers of the late 20th century. And I saw a number of critical reviews about this particular work. But some of the critics referred to some narrow philosophical issues and minor considerations found in the book (which, in my view, do not cha</span>
  <span id="freeText369481195055465667" style="display:none">I would recommend “Discipline and Punish: the Birth of the Prison” to everyone interested in the mechanisms of modern control of society through the institutions created since the end of the 18th century. <br /><br />Michel Foucault is considered one of the major philosophers of the late 20th century. And I saw a number of critical reviews about this particular work. But some of the critics referred to some narrow philosophical issues and minor considerations found in the book (which, in my view, do not change the essence of the main Foucault’s propositions). Actually, you don’t need to have a degree in philosophy or be able to juggle with different philosophical concepts and ideas in order to understand the main ideas in this work. As I said, if you are interested in how some modern institutions (or at least their certain elements) are intrinsically designed to control and discipline people and society, you will find many thoughtful insights in it...<br /><br />How did it happen that the execution of death penalty (which took most extreme and cruel forms in previous centuries) becomes more and more humanized, then in the end of the 18th - beginning of 19th centuries it is consistently removed from public space and loses it feature of a public spectacle with the imprisonment becoming the main criminal penalty? Torture becomes unacceptable, but is it because the criminal persecution system becomes more humane? Foucault’s contention is that the political and economic changes that happen in the wake of industrial and political revolutions of that period bring about the changes in legislations, judicial process, economic forms and controlling mechanisms. And the main aim is to create elaborate mechanisms of controlling and disciplining people. <br /><br />The focus of any persecution shifted from physical punishment of the body to the control, study and education of personality. The arbitrary power of the sovereign is substituted by the detailed regulation imposed by the state. <br /><br />Should it come as a surprise that the prison, while being initially designed and lauded as a means to re-educate the person and having failed to achieve that aim, is still viewed as a tool for improvement of the personality of the offender? And if it failed to achieve its educational goal, why did its model become (and still is) so widely spread and replicated in so many other social institutions (schools, army, various educational facilities, factories, working houses, etc.)? By Foucault, all these institutions are there not to improve the personality (despite what their proponents officially declared) but rather to discipline those people who enter them. The whole society, particularly with the introduction of centralized police services, become an object of comprehensive control using many of the concepts that have in its core the principles of control in prisons. <br /><br />You may have a feeling that Foucault is obsessed with social control, but he manages to provide an in-depth analysis of many social phenomena that are related to the discipline and control mechanisms. And even if you do not agree with many of his ideas, I found his analysis of those phenomena and the related mechanisms truly valuable.</span>
  <a data-text-id="369481195055465667" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1973698784" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1973698784" />
    <a title="Richard Thompson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11657479-richard-thompson"><img alt="Richard Thompson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530711053p2/11657479.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1973698784?book_show_action=true">Apr 16, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard Thompson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard Thompson" href="/user/show/11657479-richard-thompson">Richard Thompson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11657479-richard-thompson?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11657479-richard-thompson?shelf=sociology-and-anthropology">sociology-and-anthropology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1973698784" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12401009725565157435">For a long time I turned my nose up at Foucault based on what I thought that other people think that he says. It all seemed to be convoluted and beside the point, but he is so highly regarded that I decided that I should give him a chance to speak for himself, and I now confess that I was totally wrong. Foucault is brilliant. I don't always agree with him, and I think that some of his ideas need to be reexamined and evolved a bit in light of subsequent knowledge, but the core of his analysis is </span>
  <span id="freeText12401009725565157435" style="display:none">For a long time I turned my nose up at Foucault based on what I thought that other people think that he says. It all seemed to be convoluted and beside the point, but he is so highly regarded that I decided that I should give him a chance to speak for himself, and I now confess that I was totally wrong. Foucault is brilliant. I don't always agree with him, and I think that some of his ideas need to be reexamined and evolved a bit in light of subsequent knowledge, but the core of his analysis is insightful and smart. If Foucault had known, for example, about the studies of Zimbardo and Milgram, he might have approached some of his ideas from a somewhat different angle, but I think that he would still end up in more or less the same place.<br /><br />Foucault describes the history of discipline and punishment from the medieval world of the monastery and the inquisition to modern penitentiaries and ideas of deterrence and rehabilitation. He argues that throughout their history the varying systems of discipline and punishment can be best understood as different expressions of the modalities of power. As Foucault sees it, the systems evolved not to become kinder or more rational (although this may have been an incidental side effect), but to become more efficient and better able to serve the needs of those holding the levers of power in the modern world. The prison became the model for broader social mechanisms for discipline and punishment from the military to schools, hospitals and factories. These systems of organization and discipline became a key part of the mechanism of the industrial revolution and the modern world in general, which is only able to function effectively and achieve economies of scale by effective organization of masses of people. And a basic premise of all of this is that prison style mass organization can only work by objectifying the people who are cogs in the machine and is therefore a large scale violation of Kant's categorical imperative that we should always treat other people as ends not means. So the very thing that is the engine of our prosperity is antithetical to the Golden Rule. Foucault suggests that progress in science also comes out of this organizational paradigm although he never fully develops that part of his argument. Wow! No wonder a lot of people in the modern world are alienated and suicidal. It makes for depressing reading, but there's definitely something to it. <br /><br />But this is only a tiny part of what Foucault has to say. He has many more related insights which are developed in an engaging and accessibly written history of the penal system. There is a lot here to think about.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12401009725565157435" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_74511154" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/74511154" />
    <a title="Szplug" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1843184-szplug"><img alt="Szplug" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1395686229p2/1843184.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/74511154?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Szplug" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Szplug" href="/user/show/1843184-szplug">Szplug</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780394727677" href="/book/show/119552.Discipline_and_Punish">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer74511154" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6098272142446894994">This was my first exposure to Michel Foucault. I'm not sure whether it is the fault of the translator or not, but I found Foucault's prose to be rather thick and elliptical at times, to the degree that it may have contributed to the fleeting impression this work left on me. It was interesting, and presented a view on the evolution of criminal punishment that I hadn't considered in such a light before - I find, however, that much of it has already slipped away from memory.<br/><br/>The principal thrust of </span>
  <span id="freeText6098272142446894994" style="display:none">This was my first exposure to Michel Foucault. I'm not sure whether it is the fault of the translator or not, but I found Foucault's prose to be rather thick and elliptical at times, to the degree that it may have contributed to the fleeting impression this work left on me. It was interesting, and presented a view on the evolution of criminal punishment that I hadn't considered in such a light before - I find, however, that much of it has already slipped away from memory.<br /><br />The principal thrust of the book is to present a view on how the punishment of felon's has evolved in western society from brutal, pain-filled tortures and executions meant to punish the victim for their effrontery to the monarch - stressing a personal offense on the part of the transgressor - to a very systematic and abstract approach meant to remove the convict from society and allow them to be kept under observation. The core concept of this evolution is presented through a very interesting treatise on Jeremy Bentham's <i>Panopticon</i>, an early prison design in which all of the prisoner's cells were visible from a central hub - the inmates couldn't see inside this centre, so they never knew <i>when</i> they were being watched, and thus came to expect that <i>at any given moment</i> they were under observation. This control method has been extended to society, where the populace, aware of being observed by <i>the state</i> in all of their everyday actions, have modified their behavior so as not to draw attention to themselves and risk being punished. Foucault has provided a key ingredient to explaining how we have so easily fallen into a <i>conventional wisdom</i> culture, why we are so generally intolerant of eccentricity and the strange. What's more, he has also put another piece into the twentieth century origins of totalitarianism jigsaw puzzle. An educational and interesting book, if somewhat inaccessible. I'll read more Foucault in the future - I just won't be in any rush to do so.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6098272142446894994" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3182180436" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3182180436" />
    <a title="Alain Dib" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70886726-alain-dib"><img alt="Alain Dib" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507354926p2/70886726.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3182180436?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alain Dib" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alain Dib" href="/user/show/70886726-alain-dib">Alain Dib</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9782070729685" href="/book/show/846634.Surveiller_et_punir_Naissance_de_la_prison">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70886726-alain-dib?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3182180436" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7272731206598966318">"Discipline and punish: The birth of the prison" is undoubtedly a controversial book. Either people like it and they are eager to change enthusiastically the system, or at least attempt to do so. On the other hand you have people who hate heavily on it, attacking the post-modernists in general and Michel Foucault in particular. At this instant he is accused of cultural relativism, of feeling repressed having lived as a homosexual man, of claiming crazy stupid things. <br/>Most of those claims stem fr</span>
  <span id="freeText7272731206598966318" style="display:none">"Discipline and punish: The birth of the prison" is undoubtedly a controversial book. Either people like it and they are eager to change enthusiastically the system, or at least attempt to do so. On the other hand you have people who hate heavily on it, attacking the post-modernists in general and Michel Foucault in particular. At this instant he is accused of cultural relativism, of feeling repressed having lived as a homosexual man, of claiming crazy stupid things. <br />Most of those claims stem from a big misunderstanding of Foucault and are often(mostly at least) from people who neither studied philosophy or any related field which makes this work less accessible if you don't have a certain degree I would say of openness. <br />I would like to emphasize the fact that this man we're talking about isn't some stupid guy. A student of the école normale superieure(the most prestigious university of philosophy in all of french which you can't join without undertaking exams that by the way Sartre failed the first time around) isn't some stupid man you can discard by saying: well yes doesn't know what he's talking about, it doesn't make sense mate or he's downright stupid! I can reassure you: he is not.<br />Let's get this out of the way: Michel Foucault probably got a lot of things wrong.<br />But can't we acknowledge that he probably didn't get it all wrong? <br />This book is well documented and focuses mainly on a historical analysis of the penal system, how it evolved and especially the way in which we treat prisoners. For Foucault the seemingly more humane approach is in fact a facade and he proceeds to demonstrate by his standards why that is. The notion of power and coercion is important, as it is at the core of his theory. The philosopher claims that the human sciences are just a way to control people through nomenclature and classifications, that it is a new "scientific" and "improved" way of controlling certain individuals. He then attacks all instance of power. Some of them are schools, prisons, hospitals or others. It is for him the very structure of those institutions which is basically the same. He then argues that it is the system that is controlling everybody that it is the application of the panopticism advocated by Bentham. In other words, it is a continuous supervision of the individual. An individual that more often than not cannot see the one observing, this is a certain way of maintaining fear and the omnipresent presence of social oppressive forces which in return will affect the criminals psychologically. There is a certain truth to that in the era of modern surveillance where it is basically impossible to do anything without the constant feeling of being observed and judged accordingly. <br />You probably think it sounds like something a paranoid person would say? Maybe, but then again, is it completely untrue?<br />Foucault also tackles issues like the power of the king in the first part of the book. It is a rather interesting part that I will leave for the readers to discover. The philosopher is rather disappointed with the role religious groups did play in the establishment of the coercion of the people. He talks about monastic community and how the church condemned some people in the past. Also, about the protestant army and the early hand the church had on the kind of discipline insured at schools, which he join to certain religious values from which discipline stem. For him discipline is a ramification of the faith and good repartition of the time by believers that need to pray to god...<br />Foucault also points out rather rightly the role of prisoners as economy shifted from a feudal regime to the liberalism of today. Prisoners have to do more work because it made them more productive. On the other hand I personally can see why work would benefit prisoners but it's not something me and Mr Foucault see eye to eye to.<br />He's also, rather skeptical about a certain worldview where prisons don't "cure people" they are here to maintain certain people here for others to see. The delinquent is here to stay. Also there is a certain circular logic to prisons which end result id the never ending number of criminals and an impossible task to get out of the political network of forces.<br />Some aspect of his theory I'm not completely with is his attack on psychiatry and psychology which is rather justified. But here, I don't think it's just about power, for you see there are some people who are suicidal and I don't think that they will complain very much about some dude trying to help them out.<br />Foucault is lyrical in his writing which personally made me enjoy the book that much more. Some people attack him on his style which makes everything seem obscure and incomprehensible making him sound deep when he clearly isn't.<br />Personally I didn't encounter any problem concerning this particular aspect because as I said he did undergo some serious studies so I don't expect him to write like a 7th grader. Also, one time on an interview he did say that it is a kind of courtesy to the reader.<br />Is this an easy book? Not quite<br />Should you read it at least once in your life? I univocally say yes<br />Will it upset some? Yes for sure<br />If someone is interested, you can watch on youtube a video between Foucault and Chomskey having a debate. It is pretty interesting.<br />Also if you wish to know more about this man there is a lot of videos or interviews with him on youtube which are highly enjoyable as I myself am a french speaker(some have subtitles I think).</span>
  <a data-text-id="7272731206598966318" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_91819941" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/91819941" />
    <a title="Erik Graff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/974210-erik-graff"><img alt="Erik Graff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1299888977p2/974210.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/91819941?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erik Graff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erik Graff" href="/user/show/974210-erik-graff">Erik Graff</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780394727677" href="/book/show/119552.Discipline_and_Punish">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Erik by: </span>
      Erik Badger
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/974210-erik-graff?shelf=history">history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer91819941" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13708669909212072418">This book was much less personally problematic than his first book about sexuality because prisons are, barring one night as a teen, beyond my experience. It did shake up some of the beliefs I'd obtained in elementary school about Patricia Mott and the prison reforms of the nineteenth century--reforms which were naturally part of the ever-progressive movement of the world led by the United States of America according to the secular religion we were inculcated with back then.<br/><br/>It is, however, a fin</span>
  <span id="freeText13708669909212072418" style="display:none">This book was much less personally problematic than his first book about sexuality because prisons are, barring one night as a teen, beyond my experience. It did shake up some of the beliefs I'd obtained in elementary school about Patricia Mott and the prison reforms of the nineteenth century--reforms which were naturally part of the ever-progressive movement of the world led by the United States of America according to the secular religion we were inculcated with back then.<br /><br />It is, however, a fine, critical exposition of how prisons continue to fail in their ostensible purposes of deterring crime and reforming criminals, the pretensions of which have only grown over time. It is also interesting in that its perspective is more European, particularly French, than American. It is good for Americans to be reminded that there's a world out there.<br /><br />A more challenging book for me would be one titled "Discipline and Punish: The History of Pedagogy". It would range from pedagogical institutions from the home and childrearing, to schools and education.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13708669909212072418" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_93512050" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/93512050" />
    <a title="Siria" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/115473-siria"><img alt="Siria" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1332731316p2/115473.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/93512050?book_show_action=true">Mar 10, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Siria" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Siria" href="/user/show/115473-siria">Siria</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/115473-siria?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/115473-siria?shelf=french-history">french-history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/115473-siria?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/115473-siria?shelf=historiography">historiography</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer93512050" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5907821517950908611">When I finished reading this book, I broke out a tub of Ben and Jerry's Half Baked—chocolate and vanilla frozen yoghurt with brownie and cookie dough chunks seemed the only suitable reward after 300+ pages of Foucault's prose. Whether or not its his writing style or an effect of the translation, <i>Discipline and Punish</i> is a dense and at times frustratingly opaque book. That, coupled with Foucault's fondness for using minuscule, ahistorical details to justify large-scale abstractions, made this a v</span>
  <span id="freeText5907821517950908611" style="display:none">When I finished reading this book, I broke out a tub of Ben and Jerry's Half Baked—chocolate and vanilla frozen yoghurt with brownie and cookie dough chunks seemed the only suitable reward after 300+ pages of Foucault's prose. Whether or not its his writing style or an effect of the translation, <i>Discipline and Punish</i> is a dense and at times frustratingly opaque book. That, coupled with Foucault's fondness for using minuscule, ahistorical details to justify large-scale abstractions, made this a very frustrating book to read. I admired his refusal to accept conventional truths, but his arguments were never wholly convincing to me, his tendency to reify 'power' as a independent entity with agency of its own irritating, and his lack of intersectionality jarring (does society really treat the bodies of men and women in the same way? Of cis- and transgendered, ablebodied and those with disabilities?). To sum up: an important philosophical work, but his historical method sucks. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5907821517950908611" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2155500885" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2155500885" />
    <a title="Mack Hayden" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6036690-mack-hayden"><img alt="Mack Hayden" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1498610629p2/6036690.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2155500885?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mack Hayden" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mack Hayden" href="/user/show/6036690-mack-hayden">Mack Hayden</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6036690-mack-hayden?shelf=politics">politics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6036690-mack-hayden?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6036690-mack-hayden?shelf=history">history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2155500885" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14475044597175608468">Take this review with a grain of salt since I’d say about half of this book flew over my head completely. The density of the prose plus the preponderance of documents cited in giant chunks made this something of a slog for me. But when Foucault comes through loud and clear, almost in spite of himself, his insights are brilliant. The ideas in here about contemporary Western society’s obsession with ‘norms’ and the ways in which disciplinary, penal structures have woven their way into nearly every</span>
  <span id="freeText14475044597175608468" style="display:none">Take this review with a grain of salt since I’d say about half of this book flew over my head completely. The density of the prose plus the preponderance of documents cited in giant chunks made this something of a slog for me. But when Foucault comes through loud and clear, almost in spite of himself, his insights are brilliant. The ideas in here about contemporary Western society’s obsession with ‘norms’ and the ways in which disciplinary, penal structures have woven their way into nearly every facet of postmodern life are really worth taking apart. Maybe, when I’m hopefully a smarter guy someday, I can revisit this and grasp the parts that I missed or skimmed this time around. But that didn’t prevent me from really loving the majority of this seminal text.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14475044597175608468" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('VzXZv/uxcf0K5Us+MFiluWnwg2I1ffJAFhgdT8JXzrmVVyDOrxjBTBqfPB559PDqi6ez7+TBZpg2DTcr3TmBzw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('wFNwqPnIL+5Dhr2rt9175hl1+Z+VyrtkTsbtQvwi5GkCMYnZrWGfX1P8yov+cS61+yLJEkR2L7xu08cm40yrHw==')})
  }

<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/66933.The_Wretched_of_the_Earth"><img alt="The Wretched of the Earth" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1545547240l/66933._SX98_.jpg" />

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_66933'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/66933.The_Wretched_of_the_Earth?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Wretched of the Earth<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/37728.Frantz_Fanon?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Frantz Fanon<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.23 avg rating &mdash; 17,695 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1961\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer11195310904117323421\">A distinguished psychiatrist from Martinique who took part in the Algerian Nationalist Movement, Frantz Fanon was one of the most important theorists of revolutionary struggle, colonialism, and racial difference in history. Fanon\'s masterwork is a cl<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText11195310904117323421\" style=\"display:none\">A distinguished psychiatrist from Martinique who took part in the Algerian Nationalist Movement, Frantz Fanon was one of the most important theorists of revolutionary struggle, colonialism, and racial difference in history. Fanon\'s masterwork is a classic alongside Edward Said\'s Orientalism or The Autobiography of Malcolm X, and it is now available in a new translation that updates its language for a new generation of readers.\n\nThe Wretched of the Earth is a brilliant analysis of the psychology of the colonized and their path to liberation. Bearing singular insight into the rage and frustration of colonized peoples, and the role of violence in effecting historical change, the book incisively attacks the twin perils of post-independence colonial politics: the disenfranchisement of the masses by the elites on the one hand, and intertribal and interfaith animosities on the other.\n\nFanon\'s analysis, a veritable handbook of social reorganization for leaders of emerging nations, has been reflected all too clearly in the corruption and violence that has plagued present-day Africa. Nietzsche rewrites the former as a history of cruelty, exposing the central values of the Judaeo-Christian and liberal traditions - compassion, equality, justice - as the product of a brutal process of conditioning designed to domesticate the animal vitality of earlier cultures. The result is a book which raises profoundly disquieting issues about the violence of both ethics and interpretation. Nietzsche questions moral certainties by showing that religion and science have no claim to absolute truth, before turning on his own arguments in order to call their very presuppositions into question. The Genealogy is the most sustained of Nietzsche\'s later works and offers one of the fullest expressions of his characteristic concerns. Moving away from the Marxist/Freudian approaches that had concerned him earlier, Baudrillard developed in this book a theory of contemporary culture that relies on displacing economic notions of cultural production with notions of cultural expenditure.\n\nBaudrillard uses the concepts of the simulacra—the copy without an original—and simulation. These terms are crucial to an understanding of the postmodern, to the extent that they address the concept of mass reproduction and reproduceability that characterizes our electronic media culture.\n\nBaudrillard\'s book represents a unique and original effort to rethink cultural theory from the perspective of a new concept of cultural materialism, one that radically redefines It is both witness and tribute to the late theory of mind—the so-called structural theory, with its stress on aggression, indeed the death drive, as the pitiless adversary of eros.Civilization and Its Discontents is one of the last of Freud\'s books, written in the decade before his death and first published in German in 1929. In it he states his views on the broad question of man\'s place in the world, a place Freud defines in terms of ceaseless conflict between the individual\'s quest for freedom and society\'s demand for conformity.\nFreud\'s theme is that what works for civilization doesn\'t necessarily work for man. Man, by nature aggressive and egotistical, seeks self-satisfaction. But culture inhibits his instinctual drives. The result is a pervasive and familiar guilt.\nOf the various English translations of Freud\'s major works to appear in his lifetime, only one was authorized by Freud himself: The Standard Edition of the Complete Psychological Works of Sigmund Freud under the general editorship of James Strachey.\nFreud approved the overall editorial plan, specific renderings of key words and phrases, and the addition of valuable notes, from bibliographical and explanatory. Many of the translations were done by Strachey himself; the rest were prepared under his supervision. The result was to place the Standard Edition in a position of unquestioned supremacy over all other existing versions.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"1393246984624561158\" href=\"#\" <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/85812.Dialectic_of_Enlightenment"><img alt="Dialectic of Enlightenment: Philosophical Fragments" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1407813364l/85812._SX98_.jpg" />

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_85812'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/85812.Dialectic_of_Enlightenment?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Dialectic of Enlightenment: Philosophical Fragments<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/94301.Theodor_W_Adorno?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Theodor W. Adorno<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.09 avg rating &mdash; 6,131 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1944\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer1677946143585244311\">Dialectic of Enlightenment is undoubtedly the most influential publication of the Frankfurt School of Critical Theory. Written during the Second World War and circulated privately, it appeared in a printed edition in Amsterdam in 1947. \"What we had s<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText1677946143585244311\" style=\"display:none\">Dialectic of Enlightenment is undoubtedly the most influential publication of the Frankfurt School of Critical Theory. Written during the Second World War and circulated privately, it appeared in a printed edition in Amsterdam in 1947. &quot;What we had set out to do,&quot; the authors write in the Preface, &quot;was nothing less than to explain why humanity, instead of entering a truly human state, is sinking into a new kind of barbarism.&quot;Yet the work goes far beyond a mere critique of contemporary events. Historically remote developments, indeed, the birth of Western history and of subjectivity itself out of the struggle against natural forces, as represented in myths, are connected in a wide arch to the most threatening experiences of the present. The book consists in five chapters, at first glance unconnected, together with a number of shorter notes. The various analyses concern such phenomena as the detachment of science from practical life, formalized morality, the manipulative nature of entertainment culture, and a paranoid behavioral structure, expressed in aggressive anti-Semitism, that marks the limits of enlightenment. The authors perceive a common element in these phenomena, the tendency toward self-destruction of the guiding criteria inherent in enlightenment thought from the beginning. Using historical analyses to elucidate the present, they show, against the background of a prehistory of subjectivity, why the National Socialist terror was not an aberration of modern history but was rooted deeply in the fundamental characteristics of Western civilization.Adorno and Horkheimer see the self-destruction of Western reason as grounded in a historical and fateful dialectic between the domination of external nature and society. They trace enlightenment, which split these spheres apart, back to its mythical roots. Enlightenment and myth, therefore, are not irreconcilable opposites, but dialectically mediated qualities of both real and intellectual life. &quot;Myth is already enlightenment, and enlightenment reverts to mythology.&quot; This paradox is the fundamental thesis of the book.This new translation, based on the text in the complete edition of the works of Max Horkheimer, contains textual variants, commentary upon them, and an editorial discussion of the position of this work in the development of Critical Theory.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"1677946143585244311\" href=\"#\" Successfully defended in 1969 as Deleuze\'s main thesis toward his <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText7578109623487563721\" style=\"display:none\">Difference and Repetition, a brilliant exposition of the critique of identity, has come to be considered a contemporary classic in philosophy and one of Deleuze\'s most original works. Successfully defended in 1969 as Deleuze\'s main thesis toward his Doctorat d\'Etat at the Sorbonne, the work has been central in initiating the shift in French thought away from Hegel and Marx, towards Nietzsche and Freud. The text follows the development of two central concepts, those of pure difference and complex repetition. It shows how the two concepts are related - difference implying divergence and decentering, and repetition implying displacement and disguising. In its explication the work moves deftly between Hegel, Kierkegaard, Freud, Althusser, and Nietzsche to establish a fundamental critique of Western metaphysics. Difference and Repetition has become essential to the work of literary critics and philosophers alike, and this translation his been long awaited.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"7578109623487563721\" He investigates what he considered to be the simplest form of documented religion - totemism among the Aborigines of Australia. For Durkheim, studying Aboriginal religion was a way \'to yield an understanding of the religious nature of man, by showing us an essential and permanent aspect of humanity\'. The need and capacity of men and women to relate to one another socially lies at the heart of Durkheim\'s exploration, in which religion embodies the beliefs that shape our moral universe. \n\nThe Elementary Forms has been applauded and debated by sociologists, anthropologists, ethnographers, philosophers, and theologians, and continues to speak to new generations about the intriguing origin and nature of religion and society.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1260.Michel_Foucault">About Michel Foucault</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1260.Michel_Foucault"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1327342548p3/1260.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/1260.Michel_Foucault">Michel Foucault
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
4,029
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3227252137623001009">Michel Foucault was a French philosopher, social theorist and historian of ideas. He held a chair at the Collège de France with the title "History of Systems of Thought," but before he was Professor at University of Tunis, Tunisia, and then Professor at University Paris VIII. He lectured at several different Universities over the world as at the University at Buffalo, the University of California,</span>
  <span id="freeText3227252137623001009" style="display:none">Michel Foucault was a French philosopher, social theorist and historian of ideas. He held a chair at the Collège de France with the title "History of Systems of Thought," but before he was Professor at University of Tunis, Tunisia, and then Professor at University Paris VIII. He lectured at several different Universities over the world as at the University at Buffalo, the University of California, Berkeley and University of São Paulo, University of Rio de Janeiro, Brazil.<br /><br />Foucault is best known for his critical studies of social institutions, most notably psychiatry, medicine, the human sciences and the prison system, as well as for his work on the history of human sexuality. His writings on power, knowledge, and discourse have been widely influential in academic circles. In the 1960s Foucault was associated with structuralism, a movement from which he distanced himself. Foucault also rejected the poststructuralist and postmodernist labels later attributed to him, preferring to classify his thought as a critical history of modernity rooted in Immanuel Kant. Foucault's project was particularly influenced by Nietzsche, his "genealogy of knowledge" being a direct allusion to Nietzsche's "genealogy of morality". In a late interview he definitively stated: "I am a Nietzschean."<br /><br />Foucault was listed as the most cited scholar in the humanities in 2007 by the ISI Web of Science.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3227252137623001009" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
